From what I remember, the n-th roots of unity are the complex numbers that satisfy the equation z^n = 1. There are exactly n distinct roots, and they can be represented in the complex plane as points on the unit circle spaced evenly around the circle. Each root can be expressed using Euler's formula as e^(2ik/n) where k is an integer from 0 to n-1. So, the n roots are e^(2i0/n), e^(2i1/n), e^(2i2/n), ..., up to e^(2i(n-1)/n). That makes sense because when you raise each of these to the n-th power, you get e^(2ik) which is equal to 1, since e^(2i) is 1 and any integer multiple in the exponent just wraps around the circle.So, the first part is straightforward: the n n-th roots of 1 are the complex numbers e^(2ik/n) for k = 0, 1, 2, ..., n-1. :[Solution] x^{n}=1, that is, x^{n}=cos 2 k pi+i sin 2 k pi cdot(k=0, pm 1, pm 2, cdots), so by De Moivre's theorem, the n roots are:x_{k}=cos frac{2 k pi}{n}+i sin frac{2 k pi}{n} cdot(k=0,1,2, cdots, n-1)Since quad x_{k}^{n}=1 .(k=0,1,2, cdots, n-1)Therefore, x_{0}^{n}+x_{1}^{n}+cdots+x_{n-1}^{n}=underbrace{1+1+cdots+1}_{n uparrow}=n.

Combine the fractions: [ frac{cos A sin A + cos B sin B}{sin A sin B} = 2. ] This simplifies to: [ cos A sin A + cos B sin B = 2 sin A sin B. ]2. Use the double-angle identity: [ cos A sin A = frac{1}{2} sin 2A quad text{and} quad cos B sin B = frac{1}{2} sin 2B. ] Substituting these into the equation: [ frac{1}{2} (sin 2A + sin 2B) = 2 sin A sin B. ] Multiplying both sides by 2: [ sin 2A + sin 2B = 4 sin A sin B. ]3. Apply the sum-to-product identity: [ sin 2A + sin 2B = 2 sin(A + B) cos(A - B). ] Substituting this into the equation: [ 2 sin(A + B) cos(A - B) = 4 sin A sin B. ] Simplifying: [ sin(A + B) cos(A - B) = 2 sin A sin B. ]4. Use the identity for (sin(A + B)): [ sin(A + B) = sin(180^circ - C) = sin C. ] However, since we need to relate (A) and (B), we consider the equality conditions of the AM-GM inequality applied to the original equation.5. Apply AM-GM inequality: [ frac{cos A}{sin B} + frac{cos B}{sin A} geq 2 sqrt{frac{cos A cos B}{sin A sin B}}. ] Given equality holds, we have: [ frac{cos A}{sin B} = frac{cos B}{sin A}. ] This implies: [ cos A sin A = cos B sin B implies sin 2A = sin 2B. ]6. Solve (sin 2A = sin 2B): - Case 1: (2A = 2B implies A = B) - Case 2: (2A = 180^circ - 2B implies A + B = 90^circ) For Case 1, if (A = B), substituting back into the original equation gives (A = B = 45^circ), leading to (A + B = 90^circ).Thus, in both cases, we conclude (angle A + angle B = 90^circ).[boxed{angle A + angle B = 90^circ}]]

:19. Answer: 12.Let there be k clock faces in the stack. Consider any two adjacent columns. The sum of the numbers in them differs by either k or k-12s (where s is the number of clock faces for which one column has 12 and the next has 1). For k=1,2, ldots, 11, all these differences are not divisible by 12, but for k=12 they become divisible by 12.

Since AB is from (0,0) to (a,0), moving along AB, BP = 1. So P is at (1, 0).Point Q is on BC, and PQ = 1. Let's parametrize BC. Since B is (0,0) and C is (d, e), the parametric equation of BC is (td, te) where t ranges from 0 to 1. So Q can be represented as (td, te) for some t between 0 and 1.Similarly, PQ is the distance from P (1,0) to Q (td, te) which is sqrt[(td - 1)^2 + (te)^2] = 1. Therefore:sqrt[(td - 1)^2 + (te)^2] = 1Squaring both sides:(td - 1)^2 + (te)^2 = 1Expanding:td - 2td + 1 + te = 1Simplify:t(d + e) - 2td = 0Factor:t [ t(d + e) - 2d ] = 0Solutions are t = 0 or t = 2d / (d + e). Since t=0 would place Q at B, which is not allowed because PQ would be PB = 1, but Q must be on BC other than B. So t = 2d / (d + e).Therefore, coordinates of Q are (td, te) = (2d / (d + e), 2de / (d + e))Now, moving on to point R. R is on CA such that QR = 1 and RC = 1. Let's parametrize CA. :To analyze the given problem, let's break down the transformation and implications step by step:1. Transformation of the function f(x) to g(x):Given f(x) = 2cosfrac{2}{3}x, shifting this graph to the right by frac{pi}{2} units results in the function g(x). This shift can be mathematically represented as:[g(x) = 2cosleft(frac{2}{3}(x - frac{pi}{2})right) = 2cosleft(frac{2}{3}x - frac{pi}{3}right).]2. Analyzing the symmetry (Option A):To check if the graph of g(x) is symmetric about the point (-frac{pi}{2}, 0), we consider the general form of cosine function symmetry and shifts. However, the given transformation does not specifically align with this point for symmetry in the context of cosine functions. Therefore, without detailed symmetry analysis, we can infer that option A is not directly supported by the transformation.3. Comparing g(x) with g(frac{2pi}{3}) (Option B):Evaluating g(frac{2pi}{3}):[g(frac{2pi}{3}) = 2cosleft(frac{2}{3} times frac{2pi}{3} - frac{pi}{3}right) = 2cosleft(frac{pi}{3} - frac{pi}{3}right) = 2cos(0) = 2.]Since the cosine function ranges between -1 and 1, and g(x) scales this range to between -2 and 2, g(x) can indeed be equal to 2, but stating g(x) leq g(frac{2pi}{3}) for any x in mathbb{R} does not hold universally because g(x) can reach the maximum value of 2. Thus, option B is incorrect.4. Finding zeros of g(x) in (0, 5pi) (Option C):For zeros of g(x), we set 2cosleft(frac{2}{3}x - frac{pi}{3}right) = 0. This leads to solving frac{2}{3}x - frac{pi}{3} = frac{pi}{2} + kpi for x, where k in mathbb{Z}. Solving for x gives us x = frac{5pi}{4} + frac{3kpi}{2}. Within the interval (0, 5pi), this equation yields three solutions for x: frac{5pi}{4}, frac{11pi}{4}, frac{17pi}{4}. Hence, g(x) has exactly three zeros in the interval (0, 5pi), making option C correct.5. Evaluating the condition for alpha (Option D):Given g(alpha) = sqrt{3}, we have 2cosleft(frac{2}{3}alpha - frac{pi}{3}right) = sqrt{3}. This implies cosleft(frac{2}{3}alpha - frac{pi}{3}right) = frac{sqrt{3}}{2}. The equation frac{2}{3}alpha - frac{pi}{3} = -frac{pi}{6} leads to alpha = frac{pi}{4}. Substituting alpha into cos(2alpha-frac{pi}{6}) gives cosleft(2 times frac{pi}{4} - frac{pi}{6}right) = cosleft(frac{pi}{3}right) = frac{1}{2}, which does not match the condition given in option D.Therefore, after analyzing each option step by step, the correct choice is (boxed{C}), as it is the only statement supported by the transformation and properties of the function g(x).
